THIS ONE NEEDS TO BE DONE Only noticed 02/03/2020 [[nah -> RM GLM Basics]] <center> <h3> Generalised Linear Models </h3> <img src="https://i.guim.co.uk/img/media/944d242249beb30535101e834dabdb71f64f75b5/0_41_2307_1384/master/2307.jpg?width=620&quality=45&auto=format&fit=max&dpr=2&s=12802fb80431e093c631f7c02d3a3e68" width="500" height="300" alt="Come back here and take what's coming to you! I'll bite your legs off!"> </center> Well that was much easier than expected. We've made it to the realm of <abbr title= "a big class of linear models that have continuous response variable (that follows some family distribution) and continuous or categorical predictors">Generalised Linear Models</abbr>. If we play our cards right, we might just get some results here. Basically, you should have reached this point if you're interested in how a <abbr title = "numbers where there typically is no discrete boundary between variables. Can be numeric (e.g. height) or integer (e.g. counts)">continuous variable</abbr> is, or could be, changed by other (<abbr title = "numbers where there typically is no discrete boundary between variables. Can be numeric (e.g. height) or integer (e.g. counts)">continuous</abbr> or <abbr title = "when your variable falls into discrete bins e.g. categorical: either 'likes monty python' or 'why are you here' or e.g. ordinal (a fancy name for 'ordered categories') such as light level: 'low', 'medium', 'high'"> discrete</abbr>) variables. Before you go any further.. I must know.. is the continuous variable you're interested in a *count*? If so.. we need to take that into consideration...[[follow me -> poisson 1]] If not, you may continue your [[quest -> GLM Quest]]...Don't forget, if you're building lots of models, you'll need to account for [[Type II Error]] <center><h4> We are the knights who say... CHI </h4> <img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/eb/Knightni.jpg" width = "500" hieght = "300" alt="Chi doesn't quite rhyme, but imagine it does. For comedy"> </center> Do you have an interest in the relationships among more than two variables? Fetch me a [[Log linear model!->Log-linear model]] Or is it two variables? Well then, are your <abbr title = "Expected count=(sum of data in that row)×(sum of data in that column)/total data">expected cell counts</abbr> less than 5? [[Yes... ->Fisher Exact Test]] [[No -> GSquare or Chi Square]] TYPE II Error is when you detect a false negative, and arises when you make multiple comparisons -- or ask your data questions repeatedly. In our case, if we're looking at p-values, it means you might <abbr title="In other words, report significance when there isn't."> detect a rejection of the null hypothesis when there isn't one</abbr>. To avoid this, we usually correct for multiple comparisons A great, and commonly used correction for type II error is False Discovery Rate Correction (FDR). There are a few others as well, and they're well described (with tutorials!) <a href="https://rcompanion.org/rcompanion/f_01.html">here</a> <center><h4> G! Chi! G! <img src="https://66.media.tumblr.com/9433bf817b5a05d216b97cd3e146c0fb/tumblr_mg5qu8vBML1qda125o1_500.gifv" width = "500" hieght = "300" alt="We have changed our minds!"> </center></h4> Ah, well here you've got a couple of choices.. and really the path you choose is up to you. This quest has led you to either a G-test or Chi-square test. Now, both of these are <a href="https://stats.libretexts.org/Bookshelves/Applied_Statistics/Book%3A_Biological_Statistics_(McDonald)/02%3A_Tests_for_Nominal_Variables/2.03%3A_Chi-Square_Test_of_Goodness-of-Fit">very similar</a> when you have a large sample size. So the choice here is really up to you. For a walk through on G-test, click <a href="https://rcompanion.org/rcompanion/b_06.html">here</a>. For a walk through on Chi-square, click <a href="https://www.r-bloggers.com/chi-squared-test/">here</a>. Instead of R, you can also use online calculators for <a href="https://www.socscistatistics.com/tests/chisquare2/default2.aspx">Chi-square</a> or <a href="http://elem.com/~btilly/effective-ab-testing/g-test-calculator.html">G-test</a>. Else we [[meet our fate]]. <center> <h3> The Quest for the Generalised Linear Model </h3> <iframe width="384" height="320" src="https://www.youtube-nocookie.com/embed/QSo0duY7-9s?autoplay=1&start=1&end=51&modestbranding=1&autohide=1&showinfo=0&controls=0" frameborder="0" allowfullscreen></iframe> </center> <i>'Allo! 'oo is it?</i> <abbr title= "This text translated from: so you wahnt a generahlised mahdel, eh? well we ahlready hahve one o we cahn't help you. Do you even know whaht a generahlised linear mahdel ees? ahh ha ha! you do naht? eet's a speceefic set of mahdels zat ahssume your mahdels residuals ahre nahrmahlly deestreebuted, of course. Zere's ahctually a laht of ahssumptions zat you need to check fahr. But you wouldn't know zat, you engleesh peeg dahg! I fahrt een your generahl direction! As well as 'aving nahrmahl residuahls, you wouldn't know zat you ahlso need to check whethair: 1. your residuahls' vahriances ahre cahnstahnt; 2. your observahtions ahre independent; 4. you hahve high leverahge points.. wait.. ees zat ahn ahssumption? no? wait but.. " > So you want a generalised model, eh? Well we already <i> have one </i> so we can't help you. </abbr> Do you even know what a generalised linear model is? Ah ha ha! You do not? It's a specific set of models that assume your models <abbr title = "Residuals are kind of like errors in your results. It's the variance in your data not explained by the line your model draws -- the leftovers -- if your residuals are basically white noise, then your model has described most of the trends!">residuals </abbr> are normally distributed, of course. There's actually a lot of assumptions that you need to check for. But you wouldn't know that, you English pig dog! I fart in your general direction! As well as having normal residuals, you wouldn't know that you also need to check whether: 1. Your residuals' variances are constant; 2. Your observations are independent; 3. You have high leverage points.. wait.. is that an assumption? No? Wait but.. [[<b> AHHHHHHHHHHHHH..click...HHHHH..heerree...HHHHHGGHHH </b> ->continue the quest]] They were wrong. That's not actually an assumption, but you should check for it anyway. Odd that it sent him flying, we're not in the right part of the game for that... Anyway, the accent was getting annoying. [[Here -> r code GLM]], we've got some code, using the iris data, that should help you step through building a basic generalised linear model. When checking your assumptions, if you don't know what to look for -- it can be confusing. Click here to open a new window that describes what you should look for. You shouldn't panic if any assumptions are violated. As one of our old teachers explained to us as an undergraduate “Using these test to check if your models assumptions are violated is like getting in a row boat and using a ruler to measure the height of the waves to determine if they will be a problem for a cruise liner…" These are the assumptions we're interested in: <ol style="list-style-type: decimal"> <li>Do your errors follow a normal distribution?</li> <li>Is the variance of my residuals roughly constant across my fitted line?</li> <li>Are my observations independent of one another?</li> <li>(but not really an assumption) Are there any high leverage points?</li> </ol> <pre class="r"><code>plot(fit, which = 1)</code></pre> This is the residual vs fitted plot: <center> <img width="672" /> </center> It's usually used to figure out if your data meets the equality of variance assumption. Here, you want to make sure you look for a couple of things. If your predictor variable is continuous, we want to see that the residuals are equally and randomly distributed around the 0 line. Basically, you're looking to see it's white noise, and if it is -- great! Your model is capturing the patterns in your data, and your residuals are the random stuff left behind. You also want to make sure your plot isn't a weird shape, like a trumpet or a rugby ball. You want to see that it's equal across all your fitted values. If you have some curvature, like in this case, it means you've missed out on getting some pattern. Usually you get this when you're trying to fit a straight line to data that's probably got a curvy relationship. If you add a quadratic term to your model -- then this might fix this, because your line will then curve. But you should make sure it makes sense with your data and question first! Sometimes, this issue can also be 'fixed' by logging your response variable. But there are other transformations, such as <a href="https://www.statisticshowto.datasciencecentral.com/box-cox-transformation/" target =”_blank”>box-cox</a>that might be a better choice for you. It all depends on your particular dataset, but there are some interesting discussions <a href="https://www.statisticshowto.datasciencecentral.com/box-cox-transformation/" target =”_blank”>https://rcompanion.org/handbook/I_12.html</a>. <abbr title= "If you don't have this, your error term will not be accurate otherwise; it will be underestimating the errors at low and high values of your predictor and over-estimating at the mid-range, which in turn means your predictions and coefficients will not be accurate. But you don't really need to worry about this if you're not making predictions.">Why is equality of variance important to check? </abbr> This plot looks pretty darn good though, the data is evenly ranged about the 0 line and there is no strong curvature in the residuals, suggesting that we have captured most of the pattern in the data. There is a gap in our data but since we are <i> interpolating </i> and not <i>extrapolating</i> it will all be OK. Let's move on to [[Normality testing -> Normal]] We will walk you through this using the (in)famous Iris data that Fisher collected to demonstrate some of the tools he invented. The last column is a factor denoting the species of iris that was measured. Each row in this dataframe consitutes an observation (i.e. all the measurements for a single iris). To begin with it is always good to plot your variables against one another. Lets say for some obscure reason we wanted to see if sepal length was correlated with petal width… In this case we have a single response and a single predictor. width="672" /> If you have multiple variables with which to predict, then you can use <code>pairs(iris)</code> to look at them all at once. In this single predictor case the data looks like it has a positive linear relationship suggesting that an appropriate model might be: <pre class="r"><code>fit &lt;- lm(Sepal.Length ~ Petal.Width, data = iris)</code></pre> Looks like we've got a model now! Time to check [[assumptions -> GLM Assumptions]] The second plot (Normal Q-Q) checks that the errors are roughly normally distributed meaning that most of the residuals lie close to the line and a few far away. This has important implications for predictions again, but if you are only interested in p-values then it is less important as we can invoke the central limit theorem in most cases - some more reading on this here. Essentially, we want the points to lie on the fitted line. If they don’t it could be for any number of reasons. In your case it is likely due to the curvature that your model has missed but could also be fixed by a log-transformation of your response. [[Shall we look at Heteroscedasticity? -> Heteroscedasticity]] The third plot (Scale-Location plot) shows much the same as the residual v.s. fitted plot but on a standardised scale. The residual v.s. fitted and scale-location plots can be used to assess heteroscedasticity (variance changing with fitted values) as well. width="672" /> </center> This is also a better example of the kind of pattern we want to see in the first plot as it has lost the odd edges. Ah! I see the end of this quest! Let us travel to [[interpretation -> interpretGLM]] <div id="interpretation" class="section level1"> We have acheived our goal! The model meets assumptions (for the most part)! Our <i> quest for the generalised linear model </i> is almost complete! But wait! ...<b> what does it all mean </b> Let's think for a moment. We have a straight line relationship between two variables. Most of the time, with these models, we want to know a few things about that line. Luckily for us, some brave R-Knights have made this easily accessible with the <code>summary</code> function in r: <pre class="r"><code>summary(fit)</code></pre> <pre><code> Call: lm(formula = Sepal.Length ~ Petal.Width, data = iris) Residuals: Min 1Q Median 3Q Max -1.38822 -0.29358 -0.04393 0.26429 1.34521 Coefficients: Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(&gt;|t|) (Intercept) 4.77763 0.07293 65.51 &lt;2e-16 *** Petal.Width 0.88858 0.05137 17.30 &lt;2e-16 *** --- Signif. codes: 0 '***' 0.001 '**' 0.01 '*' 0.05 '.' 0.1 ' ' 1 Residual standard error: 0.478 on 148 degrees of freedom Multiple R-squared: 0.669, Adjusted R-squared: 0.6668 F-statistic: 299.2 on 1 and 148 DF, p-value: &lt; 2.2e-16</code></pre> The first row in that output is the intercept, in other words, where the line touches the y axis. Basically, the value of y when the x axis hits zero. Here, that's 4.77763. You can also see the standard error, <i>t</i> value and <i>p</i> value in the columns next to that. The <i>p</i> value, the mythical <i>p</i> value, describes the chance of observing what we did given that nothing is actually happening (i.e. "the null hypothesis"). If it's super small, then there's very little chance that nothing's going on. This can be flipped on its head and used as support for out alternative hypothesis that the intercept is not zero. Is this interesting for an intercept?! Probably not… but that depends on the question that you are asking. The next row down gives us the slope of the line. The slope tells you what change we observe in the y axis variable (<code>Sepal.Length</code>) for a single unit change (usually a value of 1) in our x axis variable (<code>Petal.Width</code>). Here, our model has estimated the slope to be 0.88858. This means that for a one unit change in <code>Petal.Width</code>, <code>Sepal.Length</code> increases by 0.88858. If we follow along this row we again find a wild <i>p</i> value! This <i>p</i> value tests whether or not the slope is significantly different from zero and it is probably much more interesting than the intercept! If it doesn't havea statistically significant difference from zero then the line has no slope, and there is no relationship between the two variables! Not the case here - as again it's very small! Does this mean a significant difference is interesting? Definitely <b> not </b > We must think, adventurer! Be an ecologist, biologist or knight of the round table. Does this information have practical significance in the system you're working in? That's the important thing! Does it mean something? The last thing to interpret is the R^2 value. This is the second line from the bottom where it says <code>Multiple R-squared: 0.669</code>. This value desribes the strength of the correlation between the two variables and ranges from -1 to 1. A value of 1 means the two are perfectly positively correlated, while a vlaue fo -1 means the two are perfectly negatively correlated. If it's 0 it means there's absolutely no correlation between your variables. But always remember, friend, do not fret -- no correlation might also be interesting! It all depends on your question... <h4>Our quest is complete, we have modelled the data. However... </h4> What you've seen here on this quest is the simple version, but there may be other issues that arise on future quests. Bear these things in mind: [[Do you need to add a curved line or more variables? -> Polynomial]] [[Do you want to know if your data falls into groups or categories? -> ANOVA]] </div> It may be that you need to allow the line to curve for it to fit your model - never fear! Linear models can do this for you too, you just add a polynomial term (i.e. a quadratic if you want a single bend, cubic if you need two bends, etc.). Funnily enough (maybe not funny, maybe just confusing!) this is different to a non-linear model. It's just a linear model with non-linear terms. They still get to be called linear models because we combine all the terms in the model linearly. This is a math thing, but you can read more about it <a href="https://stats.stackexchange.com/questions/71437/distinction-between-linear-and-nonlinear-model" target="_blank" > here </a> Want to add some more terms?! No problem! Here is some code to include more terms in your model as well as interactions! In this case our data does not have a exactly linear relationship as indicated by the strong curve in the red line. What this suggests is that the relationship between these two variables is not captured entirely captured by a straight line. We can amend this by fitting a quadratic model using: <pre class="r"><code>fit2 &lt;- lm(Petal.Length ~ poly(Petal.Width, 2), data = iris)</code></pre> The two lets the line make a single curve and the model will estimate exactly how it should curve for us! <pre class="r"><code>plot(fit2, which = 1)</code></pre> <center> <img [[I've got categorical, discrete and continuous variables in this dataset. -> PCA]] [[I only have discrete and categorical variables! -> MDS]]Let me introduce you to principle components analysis, friend. It's the way of the future. 'ere we don't 'ang on to outdated imperialist dogma which perpetuates the economic and social differences in our society. 'ere we work 'ard to solve our problems. This is a <i> dimensional compression technique </i>. Essentially, that means that when you have a dataset where each column is a variable and each row is an observation, then each of these columns represents a different dimension. Now, we're no lady of the lake -- we're just simple 'umans. We can't visualise more than three dimensions at all really. This technique, and other dimensional compression techniques, take your n-dimensional dataset (n being equal to the number of columns) and <i>tries</i> to compress it down to 1 - 3 dimensions that explain most of the variation in your data. Once you've done that, you can plot it and find some dominant trends in your data. Best part is, this avoids the <i> repression inherent in the system! </i> You wont get a <i>p</i> value here, not from me. Means you don't need to make corrections for [[multiple comparisons. ->Type II Error]] Now, lets open R and use that iris dataset again. Come! Come see us take down the system [[here! -> PCA Code 1]]ah, well that might just make things a bit tricker. I reckon what you need is a bit of multidimensional scaling. <center> <img src="https://www.davidbcalhoun.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/monty-python-holy-grail-16.jpg" width = "500" height = "300" alt="'What I object to is that you automatically treat me like an inferior! "> </center> You can take all your data that’s in multidimensional space (because there’s so many variables) and sort of smoosh it in a way that makes sense. You might ‘ave done a [[ PCA]]– it’s a bit like that. Just a simple way to visualise relationships. But don’t you oppress your data! Basically, you ask the computer – nicely! No hanging onto outdated imperialist dogmas here! – to throw all your observations up in multidimensional space in some pattern, calculate the distances between all the observations over and over again for lots of different patterns until the fit is the best it can be. It’s not so easy though – if I went around saying multidimensional scaling was, they’d put me away! There are a couple of different ways to do multidimensional scaling, but basically you focus on whether you use a non-metric or metric technique. This <a href=https://www.statisticshowto.datasciencecentral.com/multidimensional-scaling/ target =”_blank”>resource</a> does a great job of explaining the difference and when you’d want to use either one. But basically, when a <abbr title="What is euclidean distance in easy terms?"> Euclidean distance</abbr> metric does not suit your data, then you’re better off using a non-metric MDS. Both these techniques have been outlined brilliantly (with challenges! Excellent to make sure that you’ve got ratified decisions) in the coding club, <a href=https://ourcodingclub.github.io/tutorials/ordination/ target =”_blank”>here</a>. We recommend paying close attention to their notes on the <abbr title="Also known as the 'arch effect', this is when your data appears as a curve, horseshoe or arch when presented in ordination">horseshoe effect</abbr>. 'eres the Iris dataset again. <pre class="r"><code>#Iris Dataset data(iris)</code></pre> Now for a PCA I mentioned you need to have all your columns be continuous and numeric and you will notice that the last column (species) is not! In this case, we just set this last variable aside but we'll use it later, don't you worry! <pre class="r"><code>iris.pca &lt;- princomp(iris &#091,1:4 &#093) &#35 1:4 specifies that we are just using the first 4 columns, &#35 the square brackets are a way to subset the data</code></pre> 'ere we have fitted our PCA in just one short line of code. Now we should probably check a few things. The PCA is fitting a new axis through your data for each variable you input (4 in our case), but ideally only the first two (or maybe three at a stretch) capture the majority of the variation in our data, as this means we can effectively reproject our 4 dimensional data cloud as just a 2D image! We can check that with a [[summary -> PCA summary]]. 'ere we've got two ways to summarise the PCA <pre class="r"><code>summary(iris.pca, loadings = TRUE)</code></pre> <pre><code>## Importance of components: Comp.1 Comp.2 Comp.3 Comp.4 Standard deviation width="672" /></p> </center> As you can see our first axis alone explains 92.5% of the variation in our data! That means we can explain almost our entire dataset with just a single dimension! The second axis still contributes a bit, explaining 5.3% of the variation which isn't 'alf bad. So together, we could project our data in just two dimensions (i.e. make a two axis PCA graph) and have captured 97.8% of the variation we have in our data. We're doing it! The next plot is a screeplot, and shows the same stuff. The first axis is explaining the most: that's why it's highest up on that there axis. Now we 'afta [[plot the PCA]] and take a closer look... width="672" /></p> </center> <pre><code>## integer(0)</code></pre> 'ere we can see there are at least two definite groups in our data, which are clearly separated on the x axis (Comp.1 = Principle component 1). But we don't actually know what any of this means! Unless... These here axes are actually correlated with our original variables, we can look at these correlations and find out what these axes mean. <pre class="r"><code>loadings(iris.pca)</code></pre> <pre><code> Loadings: Comp.1 Comp.2 Comp.3 Comp.4 Sepal.Length 0.361 0.657 0.582 0.315 Sepal.Width 0.730 -0.598 -0.320 Petal.Length 0.857 -0.173 -0.480 Petal.Width 0.358 -0.546 0.754 Comp.1 Comp.2 Comp.3 Comp.4 SS loadings 1.00 1.00 1.00 1.00 Proportion Var 0.25 0.25 0.25 0.25 Cumulative Var 0.25 0.50 0.75 1.00</code></pre> The top table is really the important one for now. The values in this table range between -1 and 1. The numbers describe the strength and direction of the correlation with our original columns and each new principle component. So, for instance, the first PC is most strongly correlated with petal length. The strength of the correlation is 0.857, or 85.7%, meaning that petal length best describes the PC1 axis. Why might there be groups in our data like this? Maybe it has something to do with the species?! Let’s explore this idea with colour! Each species is definitely separating out along PC1 in particular! Interesting.... You want more you say? 'ow about we explore <i> how </i> these species are different? If we suspect that say, petal length, is a key difference between these species (which we should 'cus the axes correlations, or loadings, hinted at that) we can extend these plots into [[bubble plots]] by makin' the points change size with their respective petal lengths!Let's fatten these points up! <pre class="r"><code>#Prep sp &lt;- c(&quot;Setosa&quot;, &quot;Versicolor&quot;, 'n that's the end of this such exploratory analysis, maybe now you've got a couple of [[hypotheses to test -> Hypothesis testing]] , or you could follow this up with a [[MANOVA]] to see if there's a difference between them there groups. You could always colour your plots in lots of different ways before leaving! I guess you're on your way then, don't forget to always look out for when you're being repressed! The name's Dennis by the way, not that you bothered to find out. eh? You're not done yet huh! 'ere's a MANOVA for ya, but watch out - you only want to use the <i>p</i> value from the MANOVA, not the smaller ANOVAs later on (...you'll see). Too many ANOVAs can lead to problems, so instead we want to look at F values. The larger the better. Let's test whether there's a difference <pre><code> manova = manova (lm(as.matrix(iris[,1:4])~as.factor(iris&#36Species))) summary(manova,test="Pillai") Df Pillai approx F num Df den Df Pr(>F) as.factor(iris&#36Species) 2 1.1919 53.466 8 290 < 2.2e-16 Residuals 147 </pre></code> Well, we can see that there is a significant difference between species. But wait, where does that significant difference lie? You can run several ANOVAs and look at the F-values to figure that out. 'ere's a bit of code that'll make a nice table for you <pre><code> fValueSpecies = NULL &#35 The 'col in c()' bit just means to run this &#35 for the four columns in the iris dataset. for (col in c &#40 1:4 &#41) &#123 aovResults = aov&#40iris&#91,col&#93 &#41 ~ iris&#36Species) &#35 retrieve F value summaryStat = summary(aovResults) fValue = round(summaryStat&#91&#91 1&#93&#93&#93&#36&#96 F value&#96&#91 1&#93, digits = 2) row = cbind(colnames(stdData&#91&#91 col&#93&#93),fValue) fValueSpecies = rbind(fValueSpecies, row) &#125 rownames(fValueSpecies) = colnames(iris&#91,1:4&#93) </pre> </code> That'll run it for ya, and then you just call it to see the table <pre><code>fValueSpecies fValue Sepal.Length 119.26 Sepal.Width 49.16 Petal.Length 1180.16 Petal.Width 960.01 </code></pre> See the larger the F value, the more important the variable is in separating out the species. Probably petal length is most important, but petal width and sepal length is also worth taking a look at. Might be time to test some [[explicit questions -> Hypothesis testing]] if you've got a theory, and the desire..[[<h4><center> Fi(sher)! Fi(sher)! Fi(sher)! <img src="https://i.imgflip.com/2cm70p.jpg" width = "500" hiehgt = "300" alt="Small sample sizes.. like a small group of knights? "> </center></h4> Like Chi-square and G- tests, this test is useful when you want to look at differences between two categorical variables; or two kinds of classifications. However, this test, the fisher's exact test, is great when you're sample sizes are quite small. There's useful <a href="https://www.socscistatistics.com/tests/fisher/default2.aspx">online calculators</a> if you want to avoid using R, but also <a href="https://rcompanion.org/rcompanion/b_07.html">this fantstic walk-through</a> created by the R-Companion for Biological statistics. Now, fetch me a shrubbery! And while you're at it, do you want to do [[another quest? -> The Bridge]] When you've been counting things, you want to change the 'family' or distribution of your generalised linear model. Each distribution describes a.. 'scenario', let's say. And in a model, you use a distribution to figure out the probability in a particular scenario. The Poisson distribution's scenario is the number of events occurring in a given period of time -- and this lends itself to counts! Want a bit more information? <a href="https://www.dataquest.io/blog/tutorial-poisson-regression-in-r/" target ="_blank">This</a> description, and tutorial, has proved useful to us in the past. One thing you might want to consider though, is upgrading your poisson model to a negative binomial! Why? Well often with biological and ecological data our models rarely meet the assumption of the errors following a poisson distribution and are commonly under-/over-dispersed. A negative binomial includes one additional parameter to model the extra-poisson dispersion, which is a small price to pay most of the time. Moreover, it can be difficult to check for under-/over-dispersion relative to a poisson distribution so some (us included) advocate for swapping straight to the negative binomial in most cases! <a href="https://stats.stackexchange.com/questions/392591/are-over-dispersion-tests-in-glms-actually-useful" target ="_blank">Here</a> is a more detailed and useful outline of why and when though. <h3> Decisions, decisions... </h3> Deciding what you want to predict is a relatively straight forward as you simply need to choose which variable is your predictor. Choosing this will necessarily dictate whether or not you will use a classification technique (i.e. a technique that predicts which categorical group something belongs too; e.g. which species something might be) or a regression technique (i.e. a technique that predicts some continuous outcome; e.g. the size of something) – classifications are technically still regressions but there is a bit of muddying of the water with ambiguous terminology and we will stick with it as it is commonly used in machine learning, which is largely what we will be talking about here. Once you have decided what you *want* to predict, you need to figure out [[how best to predict it]]In machine learning there is a huge amount of jargon but two key terms you will come across is generative and determinative models. An intuitive explanation of the difference between the two types of models can be given with a little story… Let’s say you were a naïve biologist and you had trouble telling the difference between a European and African swallow. You didn’t want other people to have the same trouble so you decided to build a model for this purpose to let others differentiate them by taking a few measurements (e.g. wing length, body mass and beak length). To do this you could build either a generative or a discriminative model. <center><h4> Generative </h4></center> A **generative** model will take these covariates and tell you which swallow species it is, as well as providing information on the distributions of values for each of these species (i.e. the distribution of wing length, body mass and beak length for each swallow). So generative models tell which one is which and give you some information as to why the model is suggesting that you have one species over another. If you are after generative models then you can largely follow much of the [[ Hypothesis testing]] ]] branch of this tree. The one thing that we haven’t really included in that tree is a suite of models that use regularized regression. Regularised regression models (i.e. ridge, lasso and elastic net regression) are much an extension of generalized linear models, with the primary difference being that they shrink the coefficients (i.e. the value that you multiple the covariate by) in an attempt to avoid overfitting . These models were built to better deal with issues around collinearity . To fit these models you need to tune these shrinkage parameters, which sounds much more complex than it is! We will talk more about tuning later in this section. <center><h4> Discriminative </h4></center> A **discriminative** model will take these covariates and tell you which one it is but will give you little to no information about the distributions for each species. So why use them over generative models? Well they tend to be really good at discriminating between things, often (but not always) giving you much more accurate and precise predictive ability. It should also be noted that just because they don’t inform you on the distribution of your variables they can tell you about what each variable is contributing. The other advantage of these models is that they can really easily handle multiple classes when using them for classification, as classic frequentist techniques can be rather clunky on this front. Finally, they are also really good at dealing with some really uncommon splits in data (e.g. <a href="https://www.kdnuggets.com/2016/06/select-support-vector-machine-kernels.html" text ="_blank"> this resource </a>). Because predictive models are a bit more fluid, the rest of this section will be a discussion on techniques and how to use them. Click [[here -> predictive discussion]] to continue. It should be noted that generally, the way these models work is that they have a number of hyper-parameters – that is parameters which control the various aspects of the model. For instance, in the tree model, you must decide at what point the gain in predictive power is too small to justify another split, or what the optimal number of variables it should be to consider at each split. How do you decide these? Well no one expects you to know these details off the top of your head but there are quite a few ways to tune your model and even a package that can take care of all this for you (<a href="http://caret.r-forge.r-project.org/">caret</a>)! Tuning is essentially the process of figuring out what the optimal value is for each of a models ‘hyper-parameters’. Since the goal of our model is to predict something, one logical approach is to pick the model which predicts the best (which can be done with the aforementioned <a href="http://caret.r-forge.r-project.org/">caret</a> package in R). However, one thing you need to make a decision about is [[what constitutes *best*]]<h3>Prediction Error</h3> One way of determining which model is the ‘best’ is using prediction error. Its worth noting here that this can be used to both determine the optimal set of parameters for a given model type (e.g. ) but also for directly comparing how different model types (e.g. random forest vs. SVM). [[Root mean square error]] [[Mean absolute error]] [[Median absolute error]] [[Brier Scores]] This list is not exhaustive and it may well be that there is a metric which better represents what you are interested in, but that is something that you will have to work out. If you think we have missed a really critical one that you think *will be of interest to a lot of people* then please let us know! Once you've got what you think is the best model, you'll want to [[evaluate it]]. Double-click this passage to edit it.Double-click this passage to edit it.Double-click this passage to edit it.Double-click this passage to edit it.Model evaluation means that you use some metric(s) and evaluate how well your model performs at some task (i.e. prediction). Evaluating your model is a critical part of any modelling exercise. Anybody can fit a model to some data – this does not mean that the results are correct or true! As George Box said *“All models are wrong, but some are useful”* – and this step lets us check if it is useful for certain tasks, and to get some gauge on how good a job it does. However... If you used all your data to fit the model and then you try to fit an error metric you may run the risk of overfitting (don't worry, I've done this so many times. It'll be OK - you don't need to [[run away -> scared]]. Overfitting is when your model begins to capture the noise (i.e. the stuff you aren’t interested in) in your data, meaning that when you come to try and predict new data your model performs worse than it should! So how do you avoid overfitting? Well one way you can detect when you are beginning to model the noise in your data is to use [[data partitioning]].Data partitioning in its simplest form is where we split our data in two. We use one subset for ‘training’ (i.e. fit our model) and then the second subset to test our how well our model performs (i.e. calculate prediction error metrics). By withholding a portion of the data we can determine how well the model does when its presented with new data and thus whether or not the model is overfitting! This idea can be made even more robust by extending it to [[cross-validation]].Cross-validation (CV) involves splitting it into <abbr title="some number that's currently unknown to you, 'k'">K</abbr> subsets. They call these things 'folds' as well, to be fun -- I guess. Commonly, people use 5- or 10-fold CV, meaning the data is split into 5 or 10 equally sized data partitions. We then use K-1 (e.g. 4 if it is 5-fold, 9 if it is 10-fold) folds as training sets for the model and the remaining fold as a test set from which we calculate our prediction error metric(s). Then we repeat this procedure until each fold has been used as a test set! We can then take the average (or median) of each error metric to get an idea of how well the model performs! Sound like a pain in the cod-piece? <center> <img src="https://vignette.wikia.nocookie.net/montypython/images/3/39/Knights-of-the-Round-Table-monty-python-380127_800_441.jpg/revision/latest/scale-to-width-down/340?cb=20130716233258" width="500" height="300" alt="They're wearing them here. Promise."> </center> Well it isn’t, because some wonderful R-knights have developed a r package which can do this all for you (<a href="http://caret.r-forge.r-project.org/">caret</a> package<abbr title="what was it Terry Pratchett said about people who use more than one exclamation point?">!!!</abbr>). CV is not the only way to accurately guage a models predictive accuracy for new data – another approach involves boot-strapping but it is a little more arcane so we won’t cover it ourselves, but <a href="https://class.stanford.edu/c4x/HumanitiesScience/StatLearning/asset/cv_boot.pdf" text ="_blank">this resource</a> and <a href = "https://pdfs.semanticscholar.org/1937/3f43bb7456627880d50e53ab69e36f4c7607.pdf" text = "_blank">this resource </a> It’s worth noting that the <a href="http://caret.r-forge.r-project.org/">caret</a> package can use this approach as well! <h3> Predictive Models </h3> When prediction is your ultimate goal, then the nature of the beast changes entirely... <img src="https://vignette.wikia.nocookie.net/villains/images/b/b3/The_Rabbit_of_Caerbannog.jpg/revision/latest?cb=20130703144027" width = "500" hiehgt = "300" alt="Ahhh!"> </center> For starters it is important at the very start of the process you need to decide what you want to predict, what you want to understand and how you want to predict it (i.e. <abbr text ="insert def here">interpolating</abbr> or <abbr text="test">extrapolating</abbr> ). All of these will help you narrow down the incredibly wide spectrum of techniques that you can use for purpose of prediction! In fact, nearly everything else we have talked about in this adventure could be used for prediction. First, you must decide [[what you want to predict]]We need to test some hypotheses! But first, we need to think a bit about the structure of your data. So... Do you have multiple <abbr title = "the variable that depends on your other variables, or the thing that is responding to the other things"> response variables</abbr>? Is your data <abbr title= "observations of many characteristics or variables" >multivariate</abbr>? [[We must travel down this treachorous path! What horrors will it bring? -> multivariate]] Do you only have one <abbr title = "the variable that depends on your other variables, or the thing that is responding to the other things"> response variable</abbr>? Is your data <abbr title= "observations of a single characteristic or variable" >univariate</abbr>? [[This is the beginning of great things! Patsy, grab your coconuts. Let's travel!-> univariate]] [[There are so many variables I don't know what to do! Maybe I need to figure out what's going on a bit more... ->Hypothesis Generation]]
